src="/assets/images/icon-facebook.svg"/></button></div></div></div><div class="styles-module__IgAWAW__blogContent md:flex[0_0_calc(100%_-_80px)] md:max-w-[calc(100%_-_80px)] xl:flex[0_0_100%] xl:max-w-[100%] xl:ml-xs xxl:ml-3xl"><div><div class="rich-text-wrapper styles-module__IgAWAW__RichText"><p>The LYCEUM threat group targets organizations in sectors of strategic national importance, including oil and gas and possibly telecommunications. The activity observed by Secureworks® Counter Threat Unit™ (CTU) researchers focuses on obtaining and expanding access within a targeted network.</p><p>CTU™ research indicates that LYCEUM may have been active as early as April 2018. Domain registrations suggest that a campaign in mid-2018 focused on South African targets. In May 2019, the threat group launched a campaign against oil and gas organizations in the Middle East. This campaign followed a sharp uptick in development and testing of their toolkit against a public multi-vendor malware scanning service in February 2019. </p><p>Stylistically, the observed tradecraft resembles activity from groups such as COBALT GYPSY (which is related to OilRig, Crambus, and APT34) and COBALT TRINITY (also known as Elfin and APT33). However, none of the collected malware or infrastructure associated with LYCEUM has direct links to observed activity from these or other known threat groups. As of this publication, there is insufficient technical evidence to support an attribution assessment.</p><p>When CTU researchers first published information about LYCEUM to Secureworks Threat Intelligence clients, no public documentation on the group existed.&nbsp; Since then, reporting has emerged that refers to the threat group as <a tabindex="0" href="https://dragos.com/resource/hexane/">HEXANE</a>.</p><h2>The LYCEUM toolkit</h2><p>LYCEUM initially accesses an organization using account credentials obtained via <a tabindex="0" href="https://www.ncsc.gov.uk/blog-post/spray-you-spray-me-defending-against-password-spraying-attacks">password spraying</a> or brute-force attacks. Using compromised accounts, the threat actors send spearphishing emails with malicious Excel attachments to deliver the DanBot malware, which subsequently deploys post-intrusion tools. </p><p>CTU researchers have observed LYCEUM using the following tools:</p><ul><li><strong>DanBot</strong> — A first-stage remote access trojan (RAT) that uses DNS and HTTP-based communication mechanisms and provides basic remote access capability, including the abilities to execute arbitrary commands via cmd.exe and to upload and download files</li><li><strong>DanDrop</strong> — A VBA macro embedded in an Excel XLS file used to drop DanBot </li><li><strong>kl.ps1</strong> — A PowerShell-based keylogger</li><li><strong>Decrypt-RDCMan.ps1</strong> — Part of the <span style="color: windowtext;"><a tabindex="0" href="https://github.com/nettitude/PoshC2/blob/master/Modules/Decrypt-RDCMan.ps1">PoshC2</a> framework</span></li><li><strong>Get-LAPSP.ps1</strong> — A PowerView-based script from the PowerShell Empire framework</li></ul><h3>DanBot</h3><p>DanBot is written in C# using .NET Framework 2.0 and provides basic remote access capabilities. The DNS channel of DanBot's C2 protocol uses both IPv4 A records and IPv6 AAAA records for communication. The HTTP channel has evolved slightly since the early 2018 samples but retains common elements throughout.</p><p>Figure 1 shows a typo in DanBot’s hard-coded User-Agent: an ampersand after the operating system value. Other typos in the code include missing spaces between key elements and the misspelling of ‘Encoding’ in the Accept-Encoding header. The developer consistently used “Accept-Enconding” (note the extra ‘n’) in all DanBot samples analyzed by CTU researchers. This typographical error can facilitate network detection for the HTTP-based elements of the C2 protocol.</p><p><img src="https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/blt7fbf116012389b6f/6926c8e9ee15ad3d462e5452/lyceum_01.jpg" alt="lyceum_01.jpg" style="width: 80%;" data-sys-asset-uid="blt7fbf116012389b6f"><br><em>Figure 1. Early 2019 DanBot sample HTTP request. (Source: Secureworks)</em></p><h3>DanDrop</h3><p>The threat actors use this malicious macro to extract the DanBot payload from the weaponized document and then Base64-decode and install the malware using a scheduled task. The basic form and function of the macro have remained constant across analyzed samples, but the threat actors have made incremental improvements to obfuscate the macro and refactor some of the functionality.</p><h3>kl.ps1</h3><p>kl.ps1 is a custom keylogger that is written in PowerShell and leverages elements of the Microsoft .NET Core framework. It captures the window title and keystrokes on infected systems and stores them as Base64-encoded data. It is deployed using a scheduled task and a VBScript file. Figure 2 shows the command line used to run the keylogger script.</p><p><img src="https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/blt7f2ac32f7fe03f2e/6926c8ed571c860cde22d003/lyceum_02.jpg" alt="lyceum_02.jpg" style="width: 90%" data-sys-asset-uid="blt7f2ac32f7fe03f2e"><br><em>Figure 2. Reconstructed PowerShell command. (Source: Secureworks)</em></p><h3>Decrypt-RDCMan.ps1</h3><p><a tabindex="0" href="https://github.com/nettitude/PoshC2/blob/master/Modules/Decrypt-RDCMan.ps1">Decrypt-RDCMan.ps1</a> is a component of the <a tabindex="0" href="https://github.com/nettitude/PoshC2">PoshC2</a> penetration testing framework. It is used to decrypt passwords stored in the RDCMan configuration file, which stores details of servers and encrypted credentials to quickly establish remote desktop sessions. Recovered credentials could give the threat actors additional access within the environment. LYCEUM deployed this tool via DanBot approximately one hour after gaining initial access to a compromised environment.</p><h3>Get-LAPSP.ps1</h3><p>Get-LAPSP.ps1 is a PowerShell script that gathers account information from Active Directory via LDAP. It appears to contain <a tabindex="0" href="https://raw.githubusercontent.com/ericshoemaker/PowerView/master/Powerview.ps1">borrowed</a> code and has been run with an obfuscation script such as <a tabindex="0" href="https://github.com/danielbohannon/Invoke-Obfuscation">invoke-obfuscation</a>. LYCEUM deployed this tool via DanBot shortly after gaining initial access to a compromised environment.</p><h2>First stops in a new organization: HR and IT </h2><p>A malicious document (maldoc) that was uploaded to an online virus scanning repository in May 2019 contains the phrase “Industrial Systems Control Programming”. A superficial analysis of the document content might conclude that this document was intended for individuals working with industrial control systems (ICS) or operational technology (OT). However, the true content of this document is a training schedule spanning multiple departments, with ICS being first on the list.&nbsp; This focus on training aligns with LYCEUM’s targeting of executives, HR staff, and IT personnel. </p><p>LYCEUM delivers weaponized maldocs via spearphishing from the compromised accounts to the targeted executives, human resources (HR) staff, and IT personnel. The recipient is more likely to open a message if it originates from an internal address. Compromising individual HR accounts could yield information and account access that could be used in additional spearphishing operations within the targeted environment and against associated organizations. IT personnel have access to high-privilege accounts and documentation that could help the threat actors understand the environment without blindly navigating the network to find data and systems of interest.</p><p>CTU researchers identified several 2018 campaigns using a “security best practice” theme (see Figure 3).</p><p><img src="https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/bltcaf927e4cb80a8d0/6926c8f1861fe3519f141a05/lyceum_03.png" alt="lyceum_03.png" style="width:100%;" data-sys-asset-uid="bltcaf927e4cb80a8d0"><br><em>Figure 3. Phishing decoys used in 2018 LYCEUM campaign. The malicious Excel files dropped DanBot variants. (Source: Secureworks)</em></p><h3>No evidence of ICS targeting or capabilities</h3><p>Despite the initial perception that the maldoc sample was intended for ICS or OT staff, LYCEUM has not demonstrated an interest in those environments. However, CTU researchers cannot dismiss the possibility that the threat actors could seek access to OT environments after establishing robust access to the IT environment. Access to, and through, the IT environment is often a prerequisite to targeting an OT environment.</p><h2>Command and control infrastructure</h2><p>LYCEUM registered infrastructure using the PublicDomainRegistry.com, Web4Africa, and Hosting Concepts B.V. registrars. New domains appear to be registered for individual campaigns, and the threat actors generally use the domain within a few weeks of registration. LYCEUM C2 domains typically have a security or web technology theme. Figure 4 lists known and suspected LYCEUM infrastructure and associated creation and expiration data. </p><p><img src="https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/blt769a084b9dc254c1/6926c8f6a1bd3270599f6871/lyceum_04.png" alt="lyceum_04.png" style="width: 45%;" data-sys-asset-uid="blt769a084b9dc254c1"><br><em>Figure 4. List of known and suspected LYCEUM-operated domains. (Source: Secureworks)</em></p><h2>Conclusion</h2><p>LYCEUM is an emerging threat to energy organizations in the Middle East, but organizations should not assume that future targeting will be limited to this sector. Critical infrastructure organizations in particular should take note of the threat group’s tradecraft. Aside from deploying novel malware, LYCEUM’s activity demonstrates capabilities CTU researchers have observed from other threat groups and reinforces the value of a few key controls. &nbsp;Password spraying, DNS tunneling, social engineering, and abuse of security testing frameworks are common tactics, particularly from threat groups operating in the Middle East. While there are many security controls that could mitigate aspects of a LYCEUM intrusion, CTU researchers recommend the following to provide broad protection and detection capabilities that apply to a spectrum of threats:</p><ul style="list-style-type: disc;"><li>Implement multi-factor authentication (MFA) — Every corporate remote access service available on the Internet, including cloud applications such as Office 365/Outlook, external virtual private networks (VPNs), and single sign-on (SSO) pages, should require users to provide a one-time password in addition to their regular password. However, simply sending auto-enrollment emails can allow threat actors to enroll themselves using compromised accounts and continue their operations unhindered. </li><li>Increase visibility via endpoint detection, response, and logging — Incident response efforts are often hampered by a lack of visibility in the environment. This condition may be due to the absence of logs that allow network defenders to forensically piece together what happened or insufficient tools to monitor for ongoing threat actor activity. Endpoint monitoring tools are essential for detecting suspicious activity in the environment after other controls have been evaded.</li><li>Conduct preparedness exercises — Technology solutions cannot address all cybersecurity risks. Employees are both vulnerabilities and assets. Fostering a culture that focuses on security awareness and makes it easy for staff to work efficiently in a crisis reduces the overall frequency, impact, and cost of security incidents. <ul style="list-style-type: circle;"><li>Incident response — Table-top exercises can benefit organizations at different stages. Involving stakeholders from legal, public relations, and other groups across the organization provides insight about what data is and is not important and why. This training will enable staff to contact the correct people inside and outside the organization when an incident occurs.</li><li>Phishing awareness — Continuously reinforcing phishing awareness training and giving users an easy way to report suspicious messages helps to detect phishing campaigns early. Organizations should have processes for swift response and containment if a user executes a malicious payload.</li></ul></li></ul><br><h2>Threat indicators</h2><p>To mitigate exposure to this malware, CTU researchers recommend that organizations use available controls to review and restrict access using the indicators listed in Table 1. Note that IP addresses can be reallocated. The IP addresses and domains may contain malicious content, so consider the risks before opening them in a browser.</p><div class="table-responsive w-full overflow-x-auto"><table border="1" LYCEUM threat group targets organizations in sectors of strategic national importance, including oil and gas and possibly telecommunications. The activity observed by Secureworks® Counter Threat Unit™ (CTU) researchers focuses on obtaining and expanding access within a targeted network.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eCTU™ research indicates that LYCEUM may have been active as early as April 2018. Domain registrations suggest that a campaign in mid-2018 focused on South African targets. In May 2019, the threat group launched a campaign against oil and gas organizations in the Middle East. This campaign followed a sharp uptick in development and testing of their toolkit against a public multi-vendor malware scanning service in February 2019. \u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eStylistically, the observed tradecraft resembles activity from groups such as COBALT GYPSY (which is related to OilRig, Crambus, and APT34) and COBALT TRINITY (also known as Elfin and APT33). However, none of the collected malware or infrastructure associated with LYCEUM has direct links to observed activity from these or other known threat groups. As of this publication, there is insufficient technical evidence to support an attribution assessment.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhen CTU researchers first published information about LYCEUM to Secureworks Threat Intelligence clients, no public documentation on the group existed.\u0026nbsp; Since then, reporting has emerged that refers to the threat group as \u003ca tabindex=\"0\" href=\"https://dragos.com/resource/hexane/\"\u003eHEXANE\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eThe LYCEUM toolkit\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eLYCEUM initially accesses an organization using account credentials obtained via \u003ca tabindex=\"0\" href=\"https://www.ncsc.gov.uk/blog-post/spray-you-spray-me-defending-against-password-spraying-attacks\"\u003epassword spraying\u003c/a\u003e or brute-force attacks. Using compromised accounts, the threat actors send spearphishing emails with malicious Excel attachments to deliver the DanBot malware, which subsequently deploys post-intrusion tools. \u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eCTU researchers have observed LYCEUM using the following tools:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDanBot\u003c/strong\u003e — A first-stage remote access trojan (RAT) that uses DNS and HTTP-based communication mechanisms and provides basic remote access capability, including the abilities to execute arbitrary commands via cmd.exe and to upload and download files\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDanDrop\u003c/strong\u003e — A VBA macro embedded in an Excel XLS file used to drop DanBot \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ekl.ps1\u003c/strong\u003e — A PowerShell-based keylogger\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDecrypt-RDCMan.ps1\u003c/strong\u003e — Part of the \u003cspan style=\"color: windowtext;\"\u003e\u003ca tabindex=\"0\" href=\"https://github.com/nettitude/PoshC2/blob/master/Modules/Decrypt-RDCMan.ps1\"\u003ePoshC2\u003c/a\u003e framework\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eGet-LAPSP.ps1\u003c/strong\u003e — A PowerView-based script from the PowerShell Empire framework\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003ch3\u003eDanBot\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eDanBot is written in C# using .NET Framework 2.0 and provides basic remote access capabilities. The DNS channel of DanBot's C2 protocol uses both IPv4 A records and IPv6 AAAA records for communication. The HTTP channel has evolved slightly since the early 2018 samples but retains common elements throughout.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFigure 1 shows a typo in DanBot’s hard-coded User-Agent: an ampersand after the operating system value. Other typos in the code include missing spaces between key elements and the misspelling of ‘Encoding’ in the Accept-Encoding header. The developer consistently used “Accept-Enconding” (note the extra ‘n’) in all DanBot samples analyzed by CTU researchers. This typographical error can facilitate network detection for the HTTP-based elements of the C2 protocol.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/blt7fbf116012389b6f/6926c8e9ee15ad3d462e5452/lyceum_01.jpg\" alt=\"lyceum_01.jpg\" style=\"width: 80%;\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt7fbf116012389b6f\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 1. Early 2019 DanBot sample HTTP request. (Source: Secureworks)\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eDanDrop\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe threat actors use this malicious macro to extract the DanBot payload from the weaponized document and then Base64-decode and install the malware using a scheduled task. The basic form and function of the macro have remained constant across analyzed samples, but the threat actors have made incremental improvements to obfuscate the macro and refactor some of the functionality.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003ekl.ps1\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003ekl.ps1 is a custom keylogger that is written in PowerShell and leverages elements of the Microsoft .NET Core framework. It captures the window title and keystrokes on infected systems and stores them as Base64-encoded data. It is deployed using a scheduled task and a VBScript file. Figure 2 shows the command line used to run the keylogger script.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/blt7f2ac32f7fe03f2e/6926c8ed571c860cde22d003/lyceum_02.jpg\" alt=\"lyceum_02.jpg\" style=\"width: 90%\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt7f2ac32f7fe03f2e\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 2. Reconstructed PowerShell command. (Source: Secureworks)\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eDecrypt-RDCMan.ps1\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003ca tabindex=\"0\" href=\"https://github.com/nettitude/PoshC2/blob/master/Modules/Decrypt-RDCMan.ps1\"\u003eDecrypt-RDCMan.ps1\u003c/a\u003e is a component of the \u003ca tabindex=\"0\" href=\"https://github.com/nettitude/PoshC2\"\u003ePoshC2\u003c/a\u003e penetration testing framework. It is used to decrypt passwords stored in the RDCMan configuration file, which stores details of servers and encrypted credentials to quickly establish remote desktop sessions. Recovered credentials could give the threat actors additional access within the environment. LYCEUM deployed this tool via DanBot approximately one hour after gaining initial access to a compromised environment.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eGet-LAPSP.ps1\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eGet-LAPSP.ps1 is a PowerShell script that gathers account information from Active Directory via LDAP. It appears to contain \u003ca tabindex=\"0\" href=\"https://raw.githubusercontent.com/ericshoemaker/PowerView/master/Powerview.ps1\"\u003eborrowed\u003c/a\u003e code and has been run with an obfuscation script such as \u003ca tabindex=\"0\" href=\"https://github.com/danielbohannon/Invoke-Obfuscation\"\u003einvoke-obfuscation\u003c/a\u003e. LYCEUM deployed this tool via DanBot shortly after gaining initial access to a compromised environment.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eFirst stops in a new organization: HR and IT \u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eA malicious document (maldoc) that was uploaded to an online virus scanning repository in May 2019 contains the phrase “Industrial Systems Control Programming”. A superficial analysis of the document content might conclude that this document was intended for individuals working with industrial control systems (ICS) or operational technology (OT). However, the true content of this document is a training schedule spanning multiple departments, with ICS being first on the list.\u0026nbsp; This focus on training aligns with LYCEUM’s targeting of executives, HR staff, and IT personnel. \u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eLYCEUM delivers weaponized maldocs via spearphishing from the compromised accounts to the targeted executives, human resources (HR) staff, and IT personnel. The recipient is more likely to open a message if it originates from an internal address. Compromising individual HR accounts could yield information and account access that could be used in additional spearphishing operations within the targeted environment and against associated organizations. IT personnel have access to high-privilege accounts and documentation that could help the threat actors understand the environment without blindly navigating the network to find data and systems of interest.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eCTU researchers identified several 2018 campaigns using a “security best practice” theme (see Figure 3).\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/bltcaf927e4cb80a8d0/6926c8f1861fe3519f141a05/lyceum_03.png\" alt=\"lyceum_03.png\" style=\"width:100%;\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"bltcaf927e4cb80a8d0\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 3. Phishing decoys used in 2018 LYCEUM campaign. The malicious Excel files dropped DanBot variants. (Source: Secureworks)\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eNo evidence of ICS targeting or capabilities\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eDespite the initial perception that the maldoc sample was intended for ICS or OT staff, LYCEUM has not demonstrated an interest in those environments. However, CTU researchers cannot dismiss the possibility that the threat actors could seek access to OT environments after establishing robust access to the IT environment. Access to, and through, the IT environment is often a prerequisite to targeting an OT environment.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eCommand and control infrastructure\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eLYCEUM registered infrastructure using the PublicDomainRegistry.com, Web4Africa, and Hosting Concepts B.V. registrars. New domains appear to be registered for individual campaigns, and the threat actors generally use the domain within a few weeks of registration. LYCEUM C2 domains typically have a security or web technology theme. Figure 4 lists known and suspected LYCEUM infrastructure and associated creation and expiration data. \u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/blt769a084b9dc254c1/6926c8f6a1bd3270599f6871/lyceum_04.png\" alt=\"lyceum_04.png\" style=\"width: 45%;\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt769a084b9dc254c1\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 4. List of known and suspected LYCEUM-operated domains. (Source: Secureworks)\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eConclusion\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eLYCEUM is an emerging threat to energy organizations in the Middle East, but organizations should not assume that future targeting will be limited to this sector. Critical infrastructure organizations in particular should take note of the threat group’s tradecraft. Aside from deploying novel malware, LYCEUM’s activity demonstrates capabilities CTU researchers have observed from other threat groups and reinforces the value of a few key controls. \u0026nbsp;Password spraying, DNS tunneling, social engineering, and abuse of security testing frameworks are common tactics, particularly from threat groups operating in the Middle East. While there are many security controls that could mitigate aspects of a LYCEUM intrusion, CTU researchers recommend the following to provide broad protection and detection capabilities that apply to a spectrum of threats:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul style=\"list-style-type: disc;\"\u003e\u003cli\u003eImplement multi-factor authentication (MFA) — Every corporate remote access service available on the Internet, including cloud applications such as Office 365/Outlook, external virtual private networks (VPNs), and single sign-on (SSO) pages, should require users to provide a one-time password in addition to their regular password. However, simply sending auto-enrollment emails can allow threat actors to enroll themselves using compromised accounts and continue their operations unhindered. \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eIncrease visibility via endpoint detection, response, and logging — Incident response efforts are often hampered by a lack of visibility in the environment. This condition may be due to the absence of logs that allow network defenders to forensically piece together what happened or insufficient tools to monitor for ongoing threat actor activity. Endpoint monitoring tools are essential for detecting suspicious activity in the environment after other controls have been evaded.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eConduct preparedness exercises — Technology solutions cannot address all cybersecurity risks. Employees are both vulnerabilities and assets. Fostering a culture that focuses on security awareness and makes it easy for staff to work efficiently in a crisis reduces the overall frequency, impact, and cost of security incidents. \u003cul style=\"list-style-type: circle;\"\u003e\u003cli\u003eIncident response — Table-top exercises can benefit organizations at different stages. Involving stakeholders from legal, public relations, and other groups across the organization provides insight about what data is and is not important and why. This training will enable staff to contact the correct people inside and outside the organization when an incident occurs.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003ePhishing awareness — Continuously reinforcing phishing awareness training and giving users an easy way to report suspicious messages helps to detect phishing campaigns early. Organizations should have processes for swift response and containment if a user executes a malicious payload.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003ch2\u003eThreat indicators\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo mitigate exposure to this malware, CTU researchers recommend that organizations use available controls to review and restrict access using the indicators listed in Table 1. Note that IP addresses can be reallocated. The IP addresses and domains may contain malicious content, so consider the risks before opening them in a browser.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cdiv class=\"table-responsive w-full
